reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
from transformers import AutoConfig, AutoModelForCausalLM


class Model(torch.nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, model_name, config):
        super().__init__()
        self.model_name = model_name
        self.config = config
        self.model = AutoModelForCausalLM.from_pretrained(self.model_name, config=self.config)

    def forward(self, x):
        return self.model(x).logits


model_name = "EleutherAI/pythia-1.4b"
config = AutoConfig.from_pretrained(model_name)
vocab_size = config.vocab_size
sequence_length = 512
batch_size = 8


def get_inputs():
    inputs = torch.randint(0, vocab_size, (batch_size, sequence_length))
    return [inputs]


def get_init_inputs():
    return [model_name, config]

So rotary is applied to the first 32 dimensions of each head. - `query_key_value`: `nn.Linear(hidden_size, 3*hidden_size)` - `dense`: `nn.Linear(hidden_size, hidden_size)` - `bias`? `qkv` has bias=True, `dense` has bias=True. - Attention uses causal mask. - `GPTNeoXMLP`: - `dense_h_to_4h`: `nn.Linear(hidden_size, intermediate_size)` (intermediate_size = 8192 maybe? For pythia-1.4b it's 8192? Let's check: In GPT-NeoX 1.3B, intermediate_size is 8192. Actually for pythia-1.4b, let me check: The config has `intermediate_size = 8192`? I think yes. It's 4x hidden size. - `dense_4h_to_h`: `nn.Linear(intermediate_size, hidden_size)` - `act`: `nn.GELU()` So the plan is to write a custom CUDA kernel (or C++ function) that implements the full GPTNeoX layer forward. Let's call it `forward_gptneox_layer`. The C++ function signature: ```cpp at::Tensor forward_gptneox_layer( at::Tensor hidden_states, at::Tensor input_layernorm_weight, at::Tensor input_layernorm_bias, at::Tensor qkv_weight, at::Tensor qkv_bias, at::Tensor dense_weight, at::Tensor dense_bias, at::Tensor post_layernorm_weight, at::Tensor post_layernorm_bias, at::Tensor mlp_h_weight, at::Tensor mlp_h_bias, at::Tensor mlp_out_weight, at::Tensor mlp_out_bias, at::Tensor cos, at::Tensor sin); ``` Inside: 1. `residual = hidden_states;` 2. `hidden_states = at::layer_norm(hidden_states, {hidden_states.size(-1)}, input_layernorm_weight, input_layernorm_bias, 1e-5);` 3. `qkv = at::addmm(qkv_bias, hidden_states, qkv_weight.t());` // shape: [batch*seq, 3*hidden] Actually `qkv_weight` is [3*hidden, hidden] or [hidden, 3*hidden]? In `nn.Linear`, weight is [out_features, in_features]. So qkv_weight is [3*hidden, hidden]. So `at::addmm(qkv_bias, hidden_states, qkv_weight.t())` gives [batch*seq, 3*hidden]. Or use `at::linear(hidden_states, qkv_weight, qkv_bias)`. 4. Reshape qkv to `[batch, seq, 3, num_heads, head_size]`. In GPTNeoX, qkv is `[batch, seq, 3, num_heads, head_size]`. Actually the output of the linear is `[batch, seq, 3*hidden]`. Then split. Let's do: `auto qkv = at::linear(hidden_states, qkv_weight, qkv_bias);` `qkv = qkv.reshape({batch, seq, 3, num_heads, head_size});` `auto query = qkv.select(2, 0);` // [batch, seq, num_heads, head_size] `auto key = qkv.select(2, 1);` `auto value = qkv.select(2, 2);` 5. Apply rotary embeddings. In GPTNeoX, rotary is applied to query and key. The dimensions are split: first `rotary_ndims` get rotated, the rest are unchanged. The `cos` and `sin` are of shape `[seq, rotary_ndims]`. We need to apply them. We can write a custom CUDA kernel for rotary embedding. Or we can implement it with `at::` operations. A custom kernel is better because it avoids memory allocation and kernel launch overhead. Let's write a simple CUDA kernel `apply_rotary` that takes q (or k) tensor and modifies it in-place (or returns a new tensor). The shape is `[batch, seq, num_heads, head_size]`. We only rotate the first `rotary_ndims` (let's call it `rot_dim`). The formula is: ``` x1 = x[..., :rot_dim] x2 = x[..., rot_dim/2:rot_dim] // Actually standard RoPE: pairs (x_{2i}, x_{2i+1}) ``` Wait, GPTNeoX uses a specific implementation. Let's check the `transformers` code for `apply_rotary_pos_emb`. In GPTNeoX, they have: ```python def rotate_half(x): x1 = x[..., : x.shape[-1] // 2] x2 = x[..., x.shape[-1] // 2 :] return torch.cat((-x2, x1), dim=-1) def apply_rotary_pos_emb(q, k, cos, sin, position_ids): cos = cos[position_ids].unsqueeze(1) # [bs, 1, seq, rotary_dim] sin = sin[position_ids].unsqueeze(1) q_embed = (q * cos) + (rotate_half(q) * sin) k_embed = (k * cos) + (rotate_half(k) * sin) return q_embed, k_embed ``` Actually, the `cos` and `sin` in the model's `forward` are precomputed and cached. In `GPTNeoXAttention.forward`, they do: ```python cos, sin = self.rotary_emb(value, seq_len=kv_seq_len) query, key = apply_rotary_pos_emb(query_rot, key_rot, cos, sin, position_ids) ``` But `apply_rotary_pos_emb` may be imported from `transformers.models.gpt_neox.modeling_gpt_neox`. Let's check that function: ```python def rotate_half(x): """Rotates half the hidden dims of the input.""" x1 = x[..., : x.shape[-1] // 2] x2 = x[..., x.shape[-1] // 2 :] return torch.cat((-x2, x1), dim=-1) def apply_rotary_pos_emb(q, k, cos, sin, position_ids): """Applies Rotary Position Embedding to the query and key tensors.""" gather_indices = position_ids[:, None, :, None] # [bs, 1, seq, 1] gather_indices = gather_indices.repeat(1, cos.shape[1], 1, cos.shape[3]) cos = cos.squeeze(1).gather(1, gather_indices).squeeze(1) # [bs, seq, rotary_dim] sin = sin.squeeze(1).gather(1, gather_indices).squeeze(1) q_embed = (q * cos) + (rotate_half(q) * sin) k_embed = (k * cos) + (rotate_half(k) * sin) return q_embed, k_embed ``` Wait, that's for GPT-NeoX. Actually, there are two versions: one that uses `position_ids` to index into cos/sin cache. But if position_ids is just `[0,1,...,seq-1]`, we can just use the cache directly. We can compute `cos` and `sin` in Python and pass them. For simplicity, we can just pass `cos_cached` and `sin_cached` of shape `[seq, head_dim]` or `[seq, rotary_dim]`. Actually in the model, `self.rotary_emb` computes `cos_cached` and `sin_cached` of shape `[max_position_embeddings, rotary_ndims]`? Let's check. `GPTNeoXRotaryEmbedding`: ```python x = torch.arange(max_position_embeddings) freqs = ... emb = torch.cat((freqs, freqs), dim=-1) cos_cached = emb.cos()[None, None, :, :] sin_cached = emb.sin()[None, None, :, :] ``` Actually, in older transformers, `GPTNeoXRotaryEmbedding` stores `inv_freq`. In newer versions, it caches cos and sin. The shape is `[1, 1, max_position_embeddings, rotary_ndims]`? Let's check: The `GPTNeoXAttention` does: ```python cos, sin = self.rotary_emb(value, seq_len=kv_seq_len) ``` where value is a dummy tensor to get device/dtype. The returned cos and sin have shape `[1, 1, seq_len, rotary_ndims]`? Actually in `GPTNeoXRotaryEmbedding.forward`: ```python def forward(self, x, seq_len=None): if seq_len > self.max_seq_len_cached: self._set_cos_sin_cache(seq_len=seq_len, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype) return ( self.cos_cached[:, :, :seq_len, ...].to(dtype=x.dtype), self.sin_cached[:, :, :seq_len, ...].to(dtype=x.dtype), ) ``` And `_set_cos_sin_cache`: ```python t = torch.arange(seq_len, device=device, dtype=self.inv_freq.dtype) freqs = torch.einsum("i,j->ij", t, self.inv_freq) emb = torch.cat((freqs, freqs), dim=-1) self.cos_cached = emb.cos()[None, None, :, :] self.sin_cached = emb.sin()[None, None, :, :] ``` So `cos_cached` shape is `[1, 1, seq_len, rotary_ndims]`. In `apply_rotary_pos_emb`, they do: ```python cos = cos.squeeze(1).gather(1, gather_indices).squeeze(1) ``` where gather_indices is `position_ids[:, None, :, None]` repeated to `[bs, 1, seq, rotary_ndims]`. So effectively, cos is gathered by position_ids. If position_ids is just `[0,1,...,seq-1]`, then it's just `cos[:, :, position_ids, :]`. For simplicity, we can generate cos and sin of shape `[seq, rotary_ndims]` and pass them. The rotary embedding formula for the first `rotary_ndims` dimensions: ``` x1 = x[..., :rotary_ndims//2] x2 = x[..., rotary_ndims//2:rotary_ndims] x_rotated = cat([-x2, x1], dim=-1) x_embed = x * cos + x_rotated * sin ``` This is what `rotate_half` does: it splits the last dim in half and rotates. But note: `rotate_half` operates on the full `head_size`, not just `rotary_ndims`. Wait, in the code: ```python q_rot = q[..., : self.rotary_ndims] q_pass = q[..., self.rotary_ndims :] k_rot = k[..., : self.rotary_ndims] k_pass = k[..., self.rotary_ndims :] q_rot, k_rot = apply_rotary_pos_emb(q_rot, k_rot, cos, sin, position_ids) query = torch.cat((q_rot, q_pass), dim=-1) key = torch.cat((k_rot, k_pass), dim=-1) ``` So `rotate_half` is applied to `q_rot` which has shape `[..., rotary_ndims]`. So `x1 = x[..., :rotary_ndims//2]`, `x2 = x[..., rotary_ndims//2:]`. `rotary_ndims` must be even. For pythia-1.4b, `rotary_ndims = 32`, so half is 16. So we can write a kernel that takes q and k, and modifies only the first `rotary_ndims` dimensions using cos and sin. We pass cos and sin as `[seq, rotary_ndims]`. 6. After rotary, we need to transpose for SDPA. SDPA expects shapes: - query: [batch, num_heads, seq_len, head_dim] - key: [batch, num_heads, seq_len, head_dim] - value: [batch, num_heads, seq_len, head_dim] We can do `query = query.transpose(1, 2)`, etc. Then call `at::scaled_dot_product_attention(query, key, value, attn_mask=None, dropout_p=0.0, is_causal=true)`. This returns a tensor of shape `[batch, num_heads, seq, head_dim]`. 7. Transpose back and reshape: `attn_output = attn_output.transpose(1, 2).reshape({batch, seq, hidden_size})`. 8. Output dense: `attn_output = at::linear(attn_output, dense_weight, dense_bias)`. 9. Add residual: `hidden_states = residual + attn_output`. 10. Compute MLP: `residual = hidden_states;` `hidden_states = at::layer_norm(hidden_states, {hidden_size}, post_layernorm_weight, post_layernorm_bias, 1e-5);` `mlp_out = at::linear(at::gelu(at::linear(hidden_states, mlp_h_weight, mlp_h_bias)), mlp_out_weight, mlp_out_bias);` `hidden_states = residual + mlp_out;` 11. Return hidden_states. This is straightforward in C++. Now, to further speed up, we can also write the MLP as a single CUDA kernel that fuses the two linears and GELU? That's complex. But `at::linear` + `at::gelu` + `at::linear` will call into cuBLAS and elementwise kernels. That's already pretty fast. The biggest speedup will be from using `at::scaled_dot_product_attention` instead of manual matmul + softmax + matmul. On Ampere/Ada, SDPA will dispatch to FlashAttention or Memory-Efficient Attention. Will this be faster than the reference? Likely yes, because the reference uses the manual Python path without SDPA. But wait: In recent versions of `transformers`, GPTNeoXAttention might actually call `torch.nn.functional.scaled_dot_product_attention` if available? Let's check: As of transformers 4.35+, many models were updated to use SDPA. However, GPTNeoX might not have been updated. Let's check the source: In `transformers/models/gpt_neox/modeling_gpt_neox.py`, the `GPTNeoXAttention` class does: ```python query = query.view(batch_size, num_attention_heads, query_length, attn.head_size) key = key.view(batch_size, num_attention_heads, key_length, attn.head_size) value = value.view(batch_size, num_attention_heads, key_length, attn.head_size) ... attn_output, attn_weights = self._attn(query, key, value, attention_mask, head_mask) ``` And `_attn` uses manual matmul: `attn_weights = torch.matmul(query, key.transpose(-1, -2))`. It does not use SDPA. So our approach will be faster. But we also need to handle the `attention_mask` parameter. In causal LM, the attention mask is a causal mask. `at::scaled_dot_product_attention` with `is_causal=true` handles that internally. One issue: When using `at::scaled_dot_product_attention`, we need to ensure that the tensor is contiguous and on CUDA. We'll manage that. Now, we need to also implement rotary embeddings efficiently. Using `at::` operations for rotary might be okay, but it will allocate intermediate tensors and launch multiple kernels. We can write a custom CUDA kernel for applying rotary embeddings. This is a simple elementwise kernel and will be fast. Let's write a CUDA kernel `apply_rotary_kernel` that takes `query` and `key` (both contiguous, shape `[batch, seq, num_heads, head_size]`), and `cos_sin` (shape `[seq, rotary_ndims]`). Actually we need separate cos and sin. Let's pass `cos` and `sin` as `[seq, rotary_ndims]`. The kernel will loop over `b, s, h`, and for the first `rotary_ndims` dimensions, apply: ``` float x1 = q[b,s,h,d]; float x2 = q[b,s,h,d + rotary_ndims/2]; float c = cos[s,d]; float s_val = sin[s,d]; q_out[b,s,h,d] = x1 * c - x2 * s_val; q_out[b,s,h,d + rotary_ndims/2] = x1 * s_val + x2 * c; ``` Wait, the `rotate_half` is: ``` x1 = x[..., :rot_dim/2] x2 = x[..., rot_dim/2:] rotated = cat([-x2, x1], dim=-1) embed = x * cos + rotated * sin ``` Expanding: For d in [0, rot_dim/2): x1 = x[..., d] x2 = x[..., d + rot_dim/2] rotated_d = -x2 rotated_{d+rot_dim/2} = x1 So: embed_d = x1 * cos[d] - x2 * sin[d] embed_{d+rot_dim/2} = x1 * sin[d] + x2 * cos[d] Because `rotated * sin`: first half = (-x2) * sin[d], second half = x1 * sin[d]. And `x * cos`: first half = x1 * cos[d], second half = x2 * cos[d]. So sum: first half = x1 * cos[d] - x2 * sin[d]; second half = x2 * cos[d] + x1 * sin[d]. That's exactly the same as the standard complex multiplication: (x1 + i x2) * (cos + i sin) = (x1 cos - x2 sin) + i (x1 sin + x2 cos). So we can write it like that. We should write an in-place kernel or out-of-place. Since the input q and k are outputs from linear layer, we can modify them in-place. So we can launch a kernel that modifies q and k in-place. We also need to handle that rotary is applied only to the first `rotary_ndims` of each head. The rest is unchanged. So kernel signature: ```cuda __global__ void apply_rotary_kernel( float* __restrict__ q, float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ cos, const float* __restrict__ sin, int batch, int seq, int num_heads, int head_size, int rotary_ndims) ``` Grid: one block per (batch, seq, head) maybe. Or use blocks over (batch*seq*num_heads) and threads over rotary_ndims/2. Because rotary_ndims is small (32/2 = 16), we can have each thread handle one pair. Total elements to process: batch * seq * num_heads * (rotary_ndims/2). Launch with that many threads (maybe blocks of 256). Each thread reads two floats from q and k, reads cos/sin, writes back. Since the linear layer output is `[batch*seq, 3*hidden]`, we will reshape it to `[batch, seq, num_heads, head_size]` for q and k. Ensure contiguous. Alternatively, we can write the rotary kernel to operate directly on the `[batch, seq, num_heads, head_size]` layout. Now, the question is: how to pass the parameters from the model to our C++ function. We can either: - In Python, extract all parameters from `self.model` and pass them one by one to the C++ function per layer. - Or, we can store pointers in C++ by calling a setter function in `__init__`. Given the simplicity, I'll write C++ functions: - `set_layer_params(int idx, ...)` to store layer parameters in global static vectors. - `forward_full(at::Tensor input_ids)` to compute the full forward pass. But I worry about `std::vector<at::Tensor>` support in `load_inline`. To avoid that, I can define a C++ struct and store pointers? Or just pass tensors as arguments each time. Given that there are only 24 layers, we can write a Python loop that calls our C++ `forward_layer` 24 times. The overhead of 24 Python function calls is tiny. So in `ModelNew.forward`: ```python def forward(self, x): # x is input_ids # Run embeddings hidden_states = self.embed(x) for layer in self.layers: hidden_states = forward_layer(hidden_states, *layer) hidden_states = self.final_layer_norm(hidden_states) logits = at::linear(hidden_states, self.lm_head_weight, self.lm_head_bias) return logits ``` But we need to call the compiled extension. Actually, we can define two C++ functions: - `forward_layer` (the compiled function) - We can also define `forward_full` in C++ to reduce Python overhead further. But `forward_full` would require passing all layer parameters. We can pass them as a list of tensors to a single argument if vector is supported. Let me think: `load_inline` uses `torch::CppExtension` which auto-generates bindings. The `functions` list is used to generate pybind11 bindings using `m.def(func_name, &func_name, ...);`. If the C++ signature is `at::Tensor forward_full(at::Tensor input_ids, std::vector<at::Tensor> weights)`, pybind11 handles `std::vector<at::Tensor>` because pybind11 has a type caster for `std::vector<T>` if T has a caster, and `at::Tensor` does. So it should work. Actually, I'm pretty sure it works because `torch::nn::ModuleList` or other things handle vectors. But I'm not 100% sure. To be safe, I can test it or avoid it. Another approach: In Python, concatenate all weights into a few big tensors? No, that's a pain. Given the time, I think the safest is to call the compiled extension per layer. The Python loop overhead is negligible. And we can embed the `cos` and `sin` as buffers. So the architecture of `ModelNew`: ```python class ModelNew(torch.nn.Module): def __init__(self, model_name, config): super().__init__() self.model_name = model_name self.config = config # Load pretrained model to extract weights model = AutoModelForCausalLM.from_pretrained(model_name, config=config) # Extract buffers: embeddings, layer norms, qkv, dense, mlp, final norm, lm_head self.vocab_size = config.vocab_size self.hidden_size = config.hidden_size self.num_layers = config.num_hidden_layers self.num_heads = config.num_attention_heads self.head_size = self.hidden_size // self.num_heads self.intermediate_size = config.intermediate_size self.rotary_ndims = int(self.head_size * getattr(config, 'rotary_pct', 0.25)) self.max_position_embeddings = config.max_position_embeddings # Extract embed self.embed_weight = model.gpt_neox.embed_in.weight.detach() # [vocab, hidden] # Actually nn.Embedding weight is [num_embeddings, embedding_dim] # Extract final layer norm self.final_layernorm_weight = model.gpt_neox.final_layer_norm.weight.detach() self.final_layernorm_bias = model.gpt_neox.final_layer_norm.bias.detach() # Extract lm_head self.lm_head_weight = model.embed_out.weight.detach() # [vocab, hidden]? self.lm_head_bias = model.embed_out.bias.detach() if model.embed_out.bias is not None else torch.zeros(self.vocab_size, dtype=self.lm_head_weight.dtype, device=self.lm_head_weight.device) # Extract layer params self.layer_input_layernorm_weights = [] self.layer_input_layernorm_biases = [] self.layer_qkv_weights = [] self.layer_qkv_biases = [] self.layer_dense_weights = [] self.layer_dense_biases = [] self.layer_post_layernorm_weights = [] self.layer_post_layernorm_biases = [] self.layer_mlp_h_weights = [] self.layer_mlp_h_biases = [] self.layer_mlp_out_weights = [] self.layer_mlp_out_biases = [] for i in range(self.num_layers): layer = model.gpt_neox.layers[i] self.layer_input_layernorm_weights.append(layer.input_layernorm.weight.detach()) self.layer_input_layernorm_biases.append(layer.input_layernorm.bias.detach()) self.layer_qkv_weights.append(layer.attention.query_key_value.weight.detach()) self.layer_qkv_biases.append(layer.attention.query_key_value.bias.detach()) self.layer_dense_weights.append(layer.attention.dense.weight.detach()) self.layer_dense_biases.append(layer.attention.dense.bias.detach()) self.layer_post_layernorm_weights.append(layer.post_attention_layernorm.weight.detach()) self.layer_post_layernorm_biases.append(layer.post_attention_layernorm.bias.detach()) self.layer_mlp_h_weights.append(layer.mlp.dense_h_to_4h.weight.detach()) self.layer_mlp_h_biases.append(layer.mlp.dense_h_to_4h.bias.detach()) self.layer_mlp_out_weights.append(layer.mlp.dense_4h_to_h.weight.detach()) self.layer_mlp_out_biases.append(layer.mlp.dense_4h_to_h.bias.detach()) # Precompute rotary cos/sin caches # We can compute using the same logic as GPTNeoXRotaryEmbedding inv_freq = 1.0 / (10000 ** (torch.arange(0, self.rotary_ndims, 2).float() / self.rotary_ndims)) t = torch.arange(self.max_position_embeddings, dtype=inv_freq.dtype) freqs = torch.einsum("i,j->ij", t, inv_freq) emb = torch.cat((freqs, freqs), dim=-1) cos_cached = emb.cos().to(dtype=self.embed_weight.dtype)[:self.max_position_embeddings, :] sin_cached = emb.sin().to(dtype=self.embed_weight.dtype)[:self.max_position_embeddings, :] self.register_buffer('cos_cached', cos_cached) self.register_buffer('sin_cached', sin_cached) # delete the original model to free memory del model ``` Then `forward` calls the compiled extension for each layer. But we need to define the extension functions. Let's define: - `forward_embedding(input_ids, embed_weight)` maybe just use Python `torch.nn.functional.embedding`. - `forward_layer(hidden_states, ...)` - `forward_final(hidden_states, ln_w, ln_b, lm_w, lm_b)` or just use Python. Actually, we can do everything in Python except the layer computation. But the layer computation is the bulk. We can write a C++ function `forward_gptneox_layer` that does steps 1-11. To avoid the Python overhead of calling it 24 times, we could batch the loop by passing all layer params and having C++ loop over them. But passing vectors of tensors might be problematic. Let's consider alternative: we can concatenate all weights of the same type into a single big tensor, e.g., all `qkv_weights` stacked into `[num_layers, 3*hidden, hidden]`. Then pass them as a single tensor. We can do this for all parameters! That would make the signature manageable: ```cpp at::Tensor forward_gptneox( at::Tensor input_ids, at::Tensor embed_weight, at::Tensor input_layernorm_weights, // [num_layers, hidden] at::Tensor input_layernorm_biases, // [num_layers, hidden] at::Tensor qkv_weights, // [num_layers, 3*hidden, hidden] at::Tensor qkv_biases, // [num_layers, 3*hidden] ... at::Tensor final_layernorm_weight, at::Tensor final_layernorm_bias, at::Tensor lm_head_weight, at::Tensor lm_head_bias, at::Tensor cos_cached, at::Tensor sin_cached) ``` Inside, we can loop over layers and index into these stacked tensors. Would `at::index_select` inside a C++ loop be efficient enough? Yes, because it's just a view. Or we can use `slice` and `squeeze`. For each layer i, we can get `input_layernorm_weights[i]` via `input_layernorm_weights.select(0, i)`. This returns a view with no copy. This is very manageable. But the signature is still long. However, it's just a set of tensors. The auto-generated pybind11 can handle up to some number of arguments. Usually no limit. So we can define a C++ function `forward_fast` with ~15 arguments. Let's count: 1. input_ids 2. embed_weight 3. input_ln_weights [L, H] 4. input_ln_biases [L, H] 5. qkv_weights [L, 3H, H] 6. qkv_biases [L, 3H] 7. dense_weights [L, H, H] 8. dense_biases [L, H] 9. post_ln_weights [L, H] 10. post_ln_biases [L, H] 11. mlp_h_weights [L, I, H] (I=intermediate) 12. mlp_h_biases [L, I] 13. mlp_out_weights [L, H, I] 14. mlp_out_biases [L, H] 15. final_ln_weight [H] 16. final_ln_bias [H] 17. lm_head_weight [V, H] or [H, V]? In `nn.Linear`, weight is [out, in]. For lm_head, it's [vocab, hidden]. 18. lm_head_bias [V] 19. cos_cached [max_seq, rotary_ndims] 20. sin_cached [max_seq, rotary_ndims] 21. num_layers (int) 22. rotary_ndims (int) We can pass num_layers and rotary_ndims as ints. In C++, we can do: ```cpp hidden_states = at::embedding(embed_weight, input_ids); for (int i = 0; i < num_layers; ++i) { auto ln_w = input_ln_weights.select(0, i); auto ln_b = input_ln_biases.select(0, i); ... hidden_states = forward_layer(hidden_states, ln_w, ln_b, qkv_w, qkv_b, dense_w, dense_b, post_ln_w, post_ln_b, mlp_h_w, mlp_h_b, mlp_out_w, mlp_out_b, cos, sin, rotary_ndims); } hidden_states = at::layer_norm(hidden_states, {hidden_size}, final_ln_w, final_ln_b, 1e-5); logits = at::linear(hidden_states, lm_head_w, lm_head_b); return logits; ``` Inside `forward_layer`, we use `at::linear`, `at::layer_norm`, our custom rotary CUDA kernel, `at::scaled_dot_product_attention`, `at::gelu`. One issue: `at::scaled_dot_product_attention` might not be available in all PyTorch versions. But in PyTorch 2.0+, it is. The evaluation environment likely supports it. However, `at::scaled_dot_product_attention` might expect certain dtype and device. We'll ensure everything is on CUDA and fp32. Another issue: `at::embedding` is host-side. In C++, `at::embedding(embed_weight, input_ids)` works if `input_ids` is on CPU? No, both must be on the same device (CUDA). `at::embedding` works on GPU. Actually, `at::embedding` in C++ is `at::embedding(weight, indices, padding_idx, scale_grad_by_freq, sparse)`. It returns the embedding lookup. This is fine. Now, we need to write the custom CUDA kernel for rotary embeddings. Let's write it in the `cuda_sources` part of `load_inline`. `load_inline` signature: ```python ext = torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline( name="gptneox_fast", cpp_sources="...", cuda_sources="...", functions=["forward_fast"], extra_cuda_cflags=["--use_fast_math", "-O3"], extra_cflags=["-O3"], verbose=True, ) ``` In `cpp_sources`, we declare `at::Tensor forward_fast(...)` and define it. In `cuda_sources`, we define the `apply_rotary_kernel` and a host launcher `apply_rotary_cuda`. Let's write the CUDA kernel: ```cuda #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void apply_rotary_kernel( scalar_t* __restrict__ q, scalar_t* __restrict__ k, const scalar_t* __restrict__ cos, const scalar_t* __restrict__ sin, int batch, int seq, int num_heads, int head_size, int rotary_ndims) { int total = batch * seq * num_heads * (rotary_ndims / 2); int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= total) return; int half_rotary = rotary_ndims / 2; int pair = idx % half_rotary; int head = (idx / half_rotary) % num_heads; int s = (idx / (half_rotary * num_heads)) % seq; int b = idx / (half_rotary * num_heads * seq); int offset = ((b * seq + s) * num_heads + head) * head_size + pair; int offset2 = offset + half_rotary; int cos_sin_offset = s * rotary_ndims + pair; scalar_t q1 = q[offset]; scalar_t q2 = q[offset2]; scalar_t k1 = k[offset]; scalar_t k2 = k[offset2]; scalar_t c = cos[cos_sin_offset]; scalar_t s_val = sin[cos_sin_offset]; q[offset] = q1 * c - q2 * s_val; q[offset2] = q1 * s_val + q2 * c; k[offset] = k1 * c - k2 * s_val; k[offset2] = k1 * s_val + k2 * c; } ``` Wait, there is a naming conflict: `s` is sequence index, but `sin` is also `s`. Let's rename. Also, we need to ensure `q` and `k` are contiguous and in the layout `[batch, seq, num_heads, head_size]`. The linear output is `[batch*seq, 3*hidden]`. After reshape and split, we need to ensure contiguous. `at::reshape` returns a view but may not be contiguous if we split. To be safe, we can use `tensor.reshape({batch, seq, num_heads, head_size}).contiguous()`. That might incur a copy but is necessary for the kernel. However, we might avoid the copy by launching the kernel with appropriate strides. But to keep it simple, let's do contiguous. The overhead of a copy for Q and K is small compared to the linear and attention. Alternatively, we can apply rotary directly on the QKV tensor before splitting. The QKV tensor from linear is `[batch, seq, 3, num_heads, head_size]` after reshape. We can write a kernel that operates on this contiguous layout, processing q and k in place. That avoids extra copies. Let's do that. After `qkv = at::linear(hidden_states, qkv_weight, qkv_bias);` `qkv = qkv.reshape({batch, seq, 3, num_heads, head_size});` // contiguous if linear output is contiguous and reshape is possible Then we can get pointers: `scalar_t* q_ptr = qkv.data_ptr<scalar_t>() + ...`? Actually, the layout is `[batch, seq, 3, num_heads, head_size]`. The q component is at index `2` (or `0`) of the third dimension. Let's check: `qkv.select(2, 0)` gives q. We can pass `qkv.select(2, 0)` and `qkv.select(2, 1)` to the kernel. The kernel can operate on the strides of these slices. But if the slices are not contiguous (because the stride over dim-2 is large), it still works if we use proper indexing with strides. Let's just make q and k contiguous for simplicity. The cost is small. `qkv` from linear is contiguous, and `qkv.reshape({...})` is a view. If we `qkv.select(2,0).contiguous()`, it copies the q part. Similarly for k. The copy cost is `batch*seq*num_heads*head_size*4` bytes = 8*512*16*128*4 ≈ 33.5 MB per layer. Times 24 ≈ 0.8 GB of memory traffic. That is small compared to total compute. We can accept it. But to be more efficient, we can operate on the strided memory. Actually, we can write the kernel to directly index the strided QKV tensor: ```cuda // qkv layout: [B, S, 3, H, D] // strides: // q_stride_b = qkv.stride(0) // q_stride_s = qkv.stride(1) // q_stride_3 = qkv.stride(2) // etc. ``` But that requires passing stride values. The kernel would compute: ``` scalar_t* q = qkv_ptr + b * stride_b + s * stride_s + 0 * stride_3 + h * stride_h + d * stride_d; scalar_t* k = qkv_ptr + b * stride_b + s * stride_s + 1 * stride_3 + h * stride_h + d * stride_d; ``` This is doable. We can pass the strides to the kernel. But it's a bit more code. Alternatively, we can use `at::Tensor q = qkv.select(2, 0).contiguous();` and similarly for k and v. For v, we don't need contiguous for rotary, but we need it for SDPA. SDPA requires contiguous tensors for some backends, but we can make v contiguous too. So total cost: 3 copies of size 33.5 MB = 100 MB per layer, 2.4 GB total. That's still okay, but maybe we can avoid copies. A better approach: write the rotary kernel to operate directly on the strided QKV tensor. Let's do that; it's not too hard. Actually, the simplest is to realize that `qkv` after `reshape` is contiguous with shape `[B*S, 3*H*D]`. The linear output is `[B*S, 3*H*D]` and is contiguous. Reshape to `[B, S, 3, H, D]` is contiguous (since total elements match). So the stride for dimension 2 is `H*D`. So q and k are separated by `H*D` elements at the innermost dimension? No, the layout is: for each b,s, there are 3 blocks of H*D. So q is at offset 0, k at offset H*D, v at offset 2*H*D within the `[b,s, ...]` slice. The stride for the head dimension is D. The stride for d dimension is 1. So the pointer offset for q is: `base = qkv.data_ptr<scalar_t>() + ((b * seq + s) * 3 + 0) * (num_heads * head_size) + h * head_size + d` Similarly for k: `base = ... + ((b * seq + s) * 3 + 1) * (num_heads * head_size) + h * head_size + d` And v: `base = ... + ((b * seq + s) * 3 + 2) * (num_heads * head_size) + h * head_size + d` Because the shape is `[B, S, 3, H, D]`, the stride for B is `3*H*D*S`, for S is `3*H*D`, for 3 is `H*D`, for H is `D`, for D is 1. So we can launch a kernel that takes the `qkv` pointer and applies rotary to the q and k sub-tensors in-place. No copies needed. Kernel: ```cuda template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void apply_rotary_kernel( scalar_t* __restrict__ qkv, const scalar_t* __restrict__ cos, const scalar_t* __restrict__ sin, int batch, int seq, int num_heads, int head_size, int rotary_ndims) { int total = batch * seq * num_heads * (rotary_ndims / 2); int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= total) return; int half_rotary = rotary_ndims / 2; int pair = idx % half_rotary; int head = (idx / half_rotary) % num_heads; int s = (idx / (half_rotary * num_heads)) % seq; int b = idx / (half_rotary * num_heads * seq); int stride_bs = 3 * num_heads * head_size; int offset_qkv = ((b * seq + s)) * stride_bs; int offset_head = head * head_size + pair; int offset_q = offset_qkv + 0 * num_heads * head_size + offset_head; int offset_k = offset_qkv + 1 * num_heads * head_size + offset_head; int offset_k2 = offset_k + half_rotary; int offset_q2 = offset_q + half_rotary; int cos_sin_offset = s * rotary_ndims + pair; scalar_t q1 = qkv[offset_q]; scalar_t q2 = qkv[offset_q2]; scalar_t k1 = qkv[offset_k]; scalar_t k2 = qkv[offset_k2]; scalar_t c = cos[cos_sin_offset]; scalar_t s_val = sin[cos_sin_offset]; qkv[offset_q] = q1 * c - q2 * s_val; qkv[offset_q2] = q1 * s_val + q2 * c; qkv[offset_k] = k1 * c - k2 * s_val; qkv[offset_k2] = k1 * s_val + k2 * c; } ``` This modifies q and k in-place within the qkv tensor. Now, after rotary, we need to extract q, k, v for SDPA. We can do: ```cpp auto q = qkv.select(2, 0).transpose(1, 2).contiguous(); // [B, H, S, D] auto k = qkv.select(2, 1).transpose(1, 2).contiguous(); auto v = qkv.select(2, 2).transpose(1, 2).contiguous(); ``` But this will copy because `select` might not be contiguous after transpose. We can avoid copy by using `permute` and making sure it's contiguous. SDPA might accept non-contiguous tensors, but to be safe and get best performance, we should make them contiguous. The copy cost is 3 * 33.5 MB = 100MB per layer. This is acceptable. But wait, we can also write our own attention kernel to avoid copies entirely! But that's too complex. If `use_parallel_residual` is True, the structure is: ``` ln1 = input_layernorm(x) attn = attention(ln1) mlp = MLP(post_attention_layernorm(x)) x = x + attn + mlp ``` Wait, actually in `GPTNeoXLayer.forward` with `use_parallel_residual`: ```python attention_input = self.input_layernorm(hidden_states) attention_output = self.attention(attention_input, ...) mlp_output = self.mlp(self.post_attention_layernorm(hidden_states)) hidden_states = mlp_output + attention_output + hidden_states ``` If `use_parallel_residual` is False: ```python attention_input = self.input_layernorm(hidden_states) attention_output = self.attention(attention_input, ...) feed_forward_hidden_states = self.mlp(self.post_attention_layernorm(hidden_states + attention_output)) hidden_states = feed_forward_hidden_states + hidden_states + attention_output ``` Wait, let me look up the exact code. ```python class GPTNeoXLayer(nn.Module): def __init__(self, config): super().__init__() self.use_parallel_residual = config.use_parallel_residual self.input_layernorm = nn.LayerNorm(config.hidden_size, eps=config.layer_norm_eps) self.post_attention_layernorm = nn.LayerNorm(config.hidden_size, eps=config.layer_norm_eps) self.attention = GPTNeoXAttention(config) self.mlp = GPTNeoXMLP(config) def forward(self, hidden_states, attention_mask, position_ids, head_mask=None): attention_input = self.input_layernorm(hidden_states) attention_output = self.attention(attention_input, attention_mask, position_ids, head_mask) if self.use_parallel_residual: mlp_input = self.post_attention_layernorm(hidden_states) mlp_output = self.mlp(mlp_input) hidden_states = mlp_output + attention_output + hidden_states else: feed_forward_hidden_states = self.post_attention_layernorm(hidden_states + attention_output) feed_forward_hidden_states = self.mlp(feed_forward_hidden_states) hidden_states = feed_forward_hidden_states + hidden_states + attention_output return hidden_states ``` Yes, that's it. So if `use_parallel_residual` is False, the MLP input is `ln(hidden_states + attention_output)`. If True, it's `ln(hidden_states)` and outputs are added. We must replicate this exactly. Also, in the attention module, there is `rotary_emb`. The `cos` and `sin` are computed inside the attention module using `self.rotary_emb(value, seq_len=kv_seq_len)`. But we precompute them. Also, there is `position_ids`. In the reference model, if not passed, they are created as `torch.arange(seq_length, dtype=torch.long, device=input_ids.device)` and unsqueezed to `[1, seq_length]`. In the attention, they are broadcasted. Our implementation assumes position_ids is just `0..seq-1`. But there might be `attention_bias` (the alibi or something)? GPTNeoX uses rotary, not alibi. No other bias. Also, there is `head_mask`. It's None by default. We ignore it. One subtlety: The reference `model` returns `CausalLMOutputWithPast`, and `.logits` is a view or a copy? In any case, we return the logits tensor. Now, let's craft the code. First, the CUDA kernel and C++ wrapper. We need to handle the fact that the compiled extension may be loaded at module load time. The `load_inline` call should happen at module level in the Python file. Let's draft the Python file: ```python import torch from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline COUNTS_PER_LAYER = 12 cuda_sources = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void apply_rotary_kernel( scalar_t* __restrict__ qkv, const scalar_t* __restrict__ cos, const scalar_t* __restrict__ sin, int batch, int seq, int num_heads, int head_size, int rotary_ndims) { int half_rotary = rotary_ndims / 2; int total = batch * seq * num_heads * half_rotary; int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= total) return; int pair = idx % half_rotary; int head = (idx / half_rotary) % num_heads; int s = (idx / (half_rotary * num_heads)) % seq; int b = idx / (half_rotary * num_heads * seq); int stride_bs = 3 * num_heads * head_size; int base = ((b * seq + s)) * stride_bs; int offset_head = head * head_size + pair; int offset_q = base + 0 * num_heads * head_size + offset_head; int offset_k = base + 1 * num_heads * head_size + offset_head; int offset_q2 = offset_q + half_rotary; int offset_k2 = offset_k + half_rotary; int cs_off = s * rotary_ndims + pair; scalar_t q1 = qkv[offset_q]; scalar_t q2 = qkv[offset_q2]; scalar_t k1 = qkv[offset_k]; scalar_t k2 = qkv[offset_k2]; scalar_t c = cos[cs_off]; scalar_t s_val = sin[cs_off]; qkv[offset_q] = q1 * c - q2 * s_val; qkv[offset_q2] = q1 * s_val + q2 * c; qkv[offset_k] = k1 * c - k2 * s_val; qkv[offset_k2] = k1 * s_val + k2 * c; } void apply_rotary_cuda(at::Tensor qkv, at::Tensor cos, at::Tensor sin, int rotary_ndims) { int batch = qkv.size(0); int seq = qkv.size(1); int num_heads = qkv.size(3); int head_size = qkv.size(4); int half_rotary = rotary_ndims / 2; int total = batch * seq * num_heads * half_rotary; const int threads = 256; const int blocks = (total + threads - 1) / threads; AT_DISPATCH_FLOATING_TYPES(qkv.scalar_type(), "apply_rotary", ([&] { apply_rotary_kernel<scalar_t><<<blocks, threads>>>( qkv.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), cos.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), sin.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), batch, seq, num_heads, head_size, rotary_ndims); })); } """ cpp_sources = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> void apply_rotary_cuda(at::Tensor qkv, at::Tensor cos, at::Tensor sin, int rotary_ndims); at::Tensor forward_fast( at::Tensor input_ids, at::Tensor embed_weight, at::Tensor input_ln_weights, at::Tensor input_ln_biases, at::Tensor qkv_weights, at::Tensor qkv_biases, at::Tensor dense_weights, at::Tensor dense_biases, at::Tensor post_ln_weights, at::Tensor post_ln_biases, at::Tensor mlp_h_weights, at::Tensor mlp_h_biases, at::Tensor mlp_out_weights, at::Tensor mlp_out_biases, at::Tensor final_ln_weight, at::Tensor final_ln_bias, at::Tensor lm_head_weight, at::Tensor lm_head_bias, at::Tensor cos_cached, at::Tensor sin_cached, bool use_parallel_residual, float layer_norm_eps, int rotary_ndims) { auto hidden_states = at::embedding(embed_weight, input_ids); int batch = hidden_states.size(0); int seq = hidden_states.size(1); int hidden_size = hidden_states.size(2); int num_layers = input_ln_weights.size(0); int num_heads = qkv_weights.size(1) / 3 / head_size ...; // We need num_heads. } """ ``` Wait, we don't have `num_heads` directly from the shapes unless we know `head_size`. We can compute `num_heads` from qkv_weight shape: `qkv_weights` has shape `[num_layers, 3*hidden_size, hidden_size]`. But we don't know `head_size`. We can compute `num_heads = qkv_weights.size(1) / 3 / head_size`. But we don't have `head_size`. We could pass it as a parameter. Or compute from `dense_weights`: `[num_layers, hidden_size, hidden_size]`. Not helpful. Better to pass `num_heads` as an int parameter. So we add `int num_heads` to the signature. Also need `head_size = hidden_size / num_heads`. Let's update. Now, writing the whole `forward_fast` in C++: ```cpp at::Tensor forward_fast( at::Tensor input_ids, at::Tensor embed_weight, at::Tensor input_ln_weights, at::Tensor input_ln_biases, at::Tensor qkv_weights, at::Tensor qkv_biases, at::Tensor dense_weights, at::Tensor dense_biases, at::Tensor post_ln_weights, at::Tensor post_ln_biases, at::Tensor mlp_h_weights, at::Tensor mlp_h_biases, at::Tensor mlp_out_weights, at::Tensor mlp_out_biases, at::Tensor final_ln_weight, at::Tensor final_ln_bias, at::Tensor lm_head_weight, at::Tensor lm_head_bias, at::Tensor cos_cached, at::Tensor sin_cached, bool use_parallel_residual, float layer_norm_eps, int rotary_ndims, int num_heads) { auto hidden_states = at::embedding(embed_weight, input_ids); int batch = hidden_states.size(0); int seq = hidden_states.size(1); int hidden_size = hidden_states.size(2); int num_layers = input_ln_weights.size(0); int head_size = hidden_size / num_heads; for (int i = 0; i < num_layers; ++i) { auto ln1_w = input_ln_weights.select(0, i); auto ln1_b = input_ln_biases.select(0, i); auto qkv_w = qkv_weights.select(0, i); auto qkv_b = qkv_biases.select(0, i); auto d_w = dense_weights.select(0, i); auto d_b = dense_biases.select(0, i); auto ln2_w = post_ln_weights.select(0, i); auto ln2_b = post_ln_biases.select(0, i); auto mlp_h_w = mlp_h_weights.select(0, i); auto mlp_h_b = mlp_h_biases.select(0, i); auto mlp_out_w = mlp_out_weights.select(0, i); auto mlp_out_b = mlp_out_biases.select(0, i); auto residual = hidden_states; // Attention branch auto attn_input = at::layer_norm(hidden_states, {hidden_size}, ln1_w, ln1_b, layer_norm_eps); auto qkv = at::linear(attn_input, qkv_w, qkv_b); qkv = qkv.reshape({batch, seq, 3, num_heads, head_size}); // apply rotary apply_rotary_cuda(qkv, cos_cached, sin_cached, rotary_ndims); // extract q,k,v auto q = qkv.select(2, 0).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).contiguous(); auto k = qkv.select(2, 1).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).contiguous(); auto v = qkv.select(2, 2).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).contiguous(); auto attn_output = at::scaled_dot_product_attention(q, k, v, c10::nullopt, 0.0, true); attn_output = attn_output.permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).reshape({batch, seq, hidden_size}); attn_output = at::linear(attn_output, d_w, d_b); if (use_parallel_residual) { auto mlp_input = at::layer_norm(hidden_states, {hidden_size}, ln2_w, ln2_b, layer_norm_eps); auto mlp_output = at::linear(at::gelu(at::linear(mlp_input, mlp_h_w, mlp_h_b)), mlp_out_w, mlp_out_b); hidden_states = residual + attn_output + mlp_output; } else { auto ff_input = at::layer_norm(hidden_states + attn_output, {hidden_size}, ln2_w, ln2_b, layer_norm_eps); auto mlp_output = at::linear(at::gelu(at::linear(ff_input, mlp_h_w, mlp_h_b)), mlp_out_w, mlp_out_b); hidden_states = residual + attn_output + mlp_output; } } hidden_states = at::layer_norm(hidden_states, {hidden_size}, final_ln_weight, final_ln_bias, layer_norm_eps); auto logits = at::linear(hidden_states, lm_head_weight, lm_head_bias); return logits; } ``` Wait, does `at::gelu` exist? In C++ PyTorch, it's `at::gelu`. I think yes. `at::gelu(self)` or `at::gelu(self, "tanh")`. The default is `approximate="none"`? In `transformers`, GPTNeoXMLP uses `self.act = nn.GELU()` which defaults to `approximate="none"` in older versions, but in newer PyTorch it might default to `"none"` as well. Actually, `nn.GELU()` default is `approximate='none'`. So `at::gelu` is correct. But we can also override `to` and `cuda` methods, but it's easier to register buffers. Let's register each group of weights as a single stacked buffer. E.g.: ```python self.register_buffer('input_ln_weights', torch.stack([...])) self.register_buffer('input_ln_biases', torch.stack([...])) ... ``` This way, `model.cuda()` will move the stacked buffer, and all weights go to GPU. But `torch.stack` will create a contiguous tensor. This is exactly what we want for passing to C++! Now, we need to precompute `cos_cached` and `sin_cached`. We can compute them in Python and register as buffers. But note: the `rotary_ndims` and `inv_freq` might differ. Let's compute exactly as in GPTNeoXRotaryEmbedding. The standard GPTNeoXRotaryEmbedding uses: ```python inv_freq = 1.0 / (10000 ** (torch.arange(0, rotary_ndims, 2).float() / rotary_ndims)) ``` Wait, the `rotary_ndims` is the dimension that rotary applies to. In `GPTNeoXRotaryEmbedding`, they compute `inv_freq` based on `self.dim` which is `rotary_ndims`. Let's check the source: ```python class GPTNeoXRotaryEmbedding(nn.Module): def __init__(self, dim, max_position_embeddings=2048, base=10000, device=None): super().__init__() self.dim = dim inv_freq = 1.0 / (self.base ** (torch.arange(0, self.dim, 2).float().to(device) / self.dim)) self.register_buffer("inv_freq", inv_freq) ... def _set_cos_sin_cache(self, seq_len, device, dtype): self.max_seq_len_cached = seq_len t = torch.arange(self.max_seq_len_cached, device=device, dtype=self.inv_freq.dtype) freqs = torch.einsum("i,j->ij", t, self.inv_freq) emb = torch.cat((freqs, freqs), dim=-1) self.register_buffer("cos_cached", emb.cos()[None, None, :, :].to(dtype), persistent=False) self.register_buffer("sin_cached", emb.sin()[None, None, :, :].to(dtype), persistent=False) ``` So `inv_freq` is based on `rotary_ndims`. We can compute it similarly. Now, in our C++ code, we need to make sure that `cos_cached` and `sin_cached` have shape `[seq, rotary_ndims]`. In `GPTNeoXRotaryEmbedding`, they are `[1, 1, seq, rotary_ndims]`. In our Python code, we can squeeze them. This is fine because the inputs are always full sequences starting at position 0. The problem statement says `sequence_length = 512` and no `position_ids` are passed. So it's always `0..511`. Good. This means the model will compute and return `past_key_values`, which involves cloning or caching key/value states. Wait, in `GPTNeoXModel.forward`, when `use_cache=True` and `past_key_values` is None, it still computes `past_key_values` for each layer and returns them. This involves concatenating the current key/value with past ones (but there are no past ones). However, it still allocates the tuple of key/value tensors for each layer and returns them. This is extra memory traffic and time! Our C++ implementation doesn't compute or return `past_key_values`. We just compute the logits. That saves time and memory! This is a significant speedup because returning a tuple of 24 key/value tensors (each of shape `[batch, num_heads, seq, head_size]`) from the model is a lot of memory allocation and copying. For 24 layers, each k/v is 33.5 MB, so 24*2*33.5 = 1.6 GB of tensor allocations per forward. Avoiding this is huge. Wait, does PyTorch's `GPTNeoXModel` actually allocate `past_key_values` when `use_cache=True` and `past_key_values` is None? The keys and values are already computed as part of attention. The tuple just references them (no copy). But creating the tuple and returning extra tensors might still have Python overhead. However, the major cost is that the attention module returns the `present` tensor, which is just the key/value. This is just a reference to existing tensors, so almost zero cost. But if `use_cache=True`, the model might reshape and do extra operations on key/value for caching. Let's write the Python code. First, `cuda_sources` string. ```cpp cuda_sources = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void apply_rotary_kernel( scalar_t* __restrict__ qkv, const scalar_t* __restrict__ cos, const scalar_t* __restrict__ sin, int batch, int seq, int num_heads, int head_size, int rotary_ndims) { int half_rotary = rotary_ndims / 2; int total = batch * seq * num_heads * half_rotary; int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= total) return; int pair = idx % half_rotary; int head = (idx / half_rotary) % num_heads; int s = (idx / (half_rotary * num_heads)) % seq; int b = idx / (half_rotary * num_heads * seq); int stride_bs = 3 * num_heads * head_size; int base = ((b * seq + s)) * stride_bs; int offset_head = head * head_size + pair; int offset_q = base + 0 * num_heads * head_size + offset_head; int offset_k = base + 1 * num_heads * head_size + offset_head; int offset_q2 = offset_q + half_rotary; int offset_k2 = offset_k + half_rotary; int cs_off = s * rotary_ndims + pair; scalar_t q1 = qkv[offset_q]; scalar_t q2 = qkv[offset_q2]; scalar_t k1 = qkv[offset_k]; scalar_t k2 = qkv[offset_k2]; scalar_t c = cos[cs_off]; scalar_t s_val = sin[cs_off]; qkv[offset_q] = q1 * c - q2 * s_val; qkv[offset_q2] = q1 * s_val + q2 * c; qkv[offset_k] = k1 * c - k2 * s_val; qkv[offset_k2] = k1 * s_val + k2 * c; } void apply_rotary_cuda(at::Tensor qkv, at::Tensor cos, at::Tensor sin, int rotary_ndims) { int batch = qkv.size(0); int seq = qkv.size(1); int num_heads = qkv.size(3); int head_size = qkv.size(4); int half_rotary = rotary_ndims / 2; int total = batch * seq * num_heads * half_rotary; if (total == 0) return; const int threads = 256; const int blocks = (total + threads - 1) / threads; AT_DISPATCH_FLOATING_TYPES_AND_HALF(qkv.scalar_type(), "apply_rotary", ([&] { apply_rotary_kernel<scalar_t><<<blocks, threads>>>( qkv.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), cos.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), sin.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), batch, seq, num_heads, head_size, rotary_ndims); })); } """ ``` Note: `AT_DISPATCH_FLOATING_TYPES_AND_HALF` includes float and half. But we only need float. `AT_DISPATCH_FLOATING_TYPES` is fine. Now, `cpp_sources`: ```cpp cpp_sources = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> // Forward declaration of CUDA kernel launcher void apply_rotary_cuda(at::Tensor qkv, at::Tensor cos, at::Tensor sin, int rotary_ndims); at::Tensor forward_fast( at::Tensor input_ids, at::Tensor embed_weight, at::Tensor input_ln_weights, at::Tensor input_ln_biases, at::Tensor qkv_weights, at::Tensor qkv_biases, at::Tensor dense_weights, at::Tensor dense_biases, at::Tensor post_ln_weights, at::Tensor post_ln_biases, at::Tensor mlp_h_weights, at::Tensor mlp_h_biases, at::Tensor mlp_out_weights, at::Tensor mlp_out_biases, at::Tensor final_ln_weight, at::Tensor final_ln_bias, at::Tensor lm_head_weight, at::Tensor lm_head_bias, at::Tensor cos_cached, at::Tensor sin_cached, bool use_parallel_residual, double layer_norm_eps, int rotary_ndims, int num_heads) { auto hidden_states = at::embedding(embed_weight, input_ids); int batch = hidden_states.size(0); int seq = hidden_states.size(1); int hidden_size = hidden_states.size(2); int num_layers = input_ln_weights.size(0); int head_size = hidden_size / num_heads; for (int i = 0; i < num_layers; ++i) { auto ln1_w = input_ln_weights.select(0, i); auto ln1_b = input_ln_biases.select(0, i); auto qkv_w = qkv_weights.select(0, i); auto qkv_b = qkv_biases.select(0, i); auto d_w = dense_weights.select(0, i); auto d_b = dense_biases.select(0, i); auto ln2_w = post_ln_weights.select(0, i); auto ln2_b = post_ln_biases.select(0, i); auto mlp_h_w = mlp_h_weights.select(0, i); auto mlp_h_b = mlp_h_biases.select(0, i); auto mlp_out_w = mlp_out_weights.select(0, i); auto mlp_out_b = mlp_out_biases.select(0, i); auto residual = hidden_states; // Attention branch auto attn_input = at::layer_norm(hidden_states, {hidden_size}, ln1_w, ln1_b, layer_norm_eps); auto qkv = at::addmm(qkv_b, attn_input, qkv_w.t()); qkv = qkv.reshape({batch, seq, 3, num_heads, head_size}); apply_rotary_cuda(qkv, cos_cached, sin_cached, rotary_ndims); auto q = qkv.select(2, 0).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).contiguous(); auto k = qkv.select(2, 1).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).contiguous(); auto v = qkv.select(2, 2).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).contiguous(); auto attn_out = at::scaled_dot_product_attention(q, k, v, c10::nullopt, 0.0, true); attn_out = attn_out.permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).reshape({batch, seq, hidden_size}); attn_out = at::addmm(d_b, attn_out, d_w.t()); if (use_parallel_residual) { auto mlp_input = at::layer_norm(hidden_states, {hidden_size}, ln2_w, ln2_b, layer_norm_eps); auto mlp_h = at::addmm(mlp_h_b, mlp_input, mlp_h_w.t()); auto mlp_h_act = at::gelu(mlp_h); auto mlp_out = at::addmm(mlp_out_b, mlp_h_act, mlp_out_w.t()); hidden_states = residual + attn_out + mlp_out; } else { auto ff_input = at::layer_norm(residual + attn_out, {hidden_size}, ln2_w, ln2_b, layer_norm_eps); auto mlp_h = at::addmm(mlp_h_b, ff_input, mlp_h_w.t()); auto mlp_h_act = at::gelu(mlp_h); auto mlp_out = at::addmm(mlp_out_b, mlp_h_act, mlp_out_w.t()); hidden_states = residual + attn_out + mlp_out; } } hidden_states = at::layer_norm(hidden_states, {hidden_size}, final_ln_weight, final_ln_bias, layer_norm_eps); auto logits = at::addmm(lm_head_bias, hidden_states, lm_head_weight.t()); return logits; } """ ``` Wait, I used `lm_head_bias` and `lm_head_weight` but the variable names in signature are `lm_head_weight` and `lm_head_bias`. In the function, the parameter is `lm_head_bias`, but the local variable is `lm_head_bias` (same). That's fine. Also `final_ln_weight` vs `final_ln_weight`. OK. One thing: `at::layer_norm` signature in C++ might expect `c10::optional<Tensor>` for weight and bias, but we pass tensors directly. The overload should accept `const Tensor&`. Let's double-check. Actually, the signature is: ```cpp Tensor layer_norm(const Tensor& input, IntArrayRef normalized_shape, const Tensor& weight={}, const Tensor& bias={}, double eps=1e-5, bool cudnn_enabled=true) ``` Yes, so `at::layer_norm(hidden_states, {hidden_size}, ln1_w, ln1_b, layer_norm_eps)` works. Another thing: `use_parallel_residual`. In the non-parallel case, the MLP input is `layer_norm(residual + attn_out)`. In the reference: ```python feed_forward_hidden_states = self.post_attention_layernorm(hidden_states + attention_output) feed_forward_hidden_states = self.mlp(feed_forward_hidden_states) hidden_states = feed_forward_hidden_states + hidden_states + attention_output ``` So `hidden_states = mlp_output + residual + attn_out`. My code does exactly that: `residual + attn_out + mlp_out`. Since addition is commutative, it's the same. But order matters for numerical equivalence? In floating point, `(a + b) + c` vs `a + (b + c)` can differ slightly. However, my code does `residual + attn_out + mlp_out`, which is left-associative: `(residual + attn_out) + mlp_out`. The reference does `mlp_out + hidden_states + attention_output` which is `(mlp_out + hidden_states) + attention_output`. Wait, `hidden_states` here is the original residual. So it's `(mlp_out + residual) + attn_out`. This is slightly different from `(residual + attn_out) + mlp_out`. To be numerically equivalent within 1e-4, we should probably match the exact order. But the difference is tiny. However, to be safe, we can compute exactly as the reference. For parallel residual: ```python mlp_output = self.mlp(self.post_attention_layernorm(hidden_states)) hidden_states = mlp_output + attention_output + hidden_states ``` If `hidden_states` is the original residual, then order is `mlp_out + attn_out + residual`. My code does `residual + attn_out + mlp_out`. Again order differs. Let's write it exactly as reference: for parallel, `hidden_states = mlp_out + attn_out + residual`. For non-parallel, `hidden_states = mlp_out + residual + attn_out`. Actually, in `transformers`, `mlp_output + attention_output + hidden_states` is left-associative: `(mlp_output + attention_output) + hidden_states`. And `feed_forward_hidden_states + hidden_states + attention_output` is `(feed_forward_hidden_states + hidden_states) + attention_output`. So we should: - Parallel: `hidden_states = mlp_out + attn_out + residual;` - Non-parallel: `hidden_states = mlp_out + residual + attn_out;` Let's write it that way. Also, note that in the non-parallel case, `residual` is the original `hidden_states` before attention. After `residual + attn_out`, we pass it to `layer_norm` for MLP. So the MLP input is `ln(residual + attn_out)`. Our code: `ff_input = at::layer_norm(residual + attn_out, ...)` is correct. Now, another nuance: The reference `GPTNeoXAttention` uses `query_rot = query[..., :self.rotary_ndims]` and `key_rot = key[..., :self.rotary_ndims]`. Our rotary kernel modifies only the first `rotary_ndims` elements. That's correct. One more nuance: In `GPTNeoXAttention`, after applying rotary, they might transpose query and key before matmul. In our code, we permute to `[B, H, S, D]`. Good. Now, in the reference, the attention output is computed and then `dense` is applied. `dense` is `[hidden_size, hidden_size]`. Let's craft the Python code carefully. ```python import torch from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_sources = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void apply_rotary_kernel( scalar_t* __restrict__ qkv, const scalar_t* __restrict__ cos, const scalar_t* __restrict__ sin, int batch, int seq, int num_heads, int head_size, int rotary_ndims) { int half_rotary = rotary_ndims / 2; int total = batch * seq * num_heads * half_rotary; int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= total) return; int pair = idx % half_rotary; int head = (idx / half_rotary) % num_heads; int s = (idx / (half_rotary * num_heads)) % seq; int b = idx / (half_rotary * num_heads * seq); int stride_bs = 3 * num_heads * head_size; int base = ((b * seq + s)) * stride_bs; int offset_head = head * head_size + pair; int offset_q = base + 0 * num_heads * head_size + offset_head; int offset_k = base + 1 * num_heads * head_size + offset_head; int offset_q2 = offset_q + half_rotary; int offset_k2 = offset_k + half_rotary; int cs_off = s * rotary_ndims + pair; scalar_t q1 = qkv[offset_q]; scalar_t q2 = qkv[offset_q2]; scalar_t k1 = qkv[offset_k]; scalar_t k2 = qkv[offset_k2]; scalar_t c = cos[cs_off]; scalar_t s_val = sin[cs_off]; qkv[offset_q] = q1 * c - q2 * s_val; qkv[offset_q2] = q1 * s_val + q2 * c; qkv[offset_k] = k1 * c - k2 * s_val; qkv[offset_k2] = k1 * s_val + k2 * c; } void apply_rotary_cuda(at::Tensor qkv, at::Tensor cos, at::Tensor sin, int rotary_ndims) { int batch = qkv.size(0); int seq = qkv.size(1); int num_heads = qkv.size(3); int head_size = qkv.size(4); int half_rotary = rotary_ndims / 2; int total = batch * seq * num_heads * half_rotary; if (total == 0) return; const int threads = 256; const int blocks = (total + threads - 1) / threads; AT_DISPATCH_FLOATING_TYPES(qkv.scalar_type(), "apply_rotary", ([&] { apply_rotary_kernel<scalar_t><<<blocks, threads>>>( qkv.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), cos.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), sin.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), batch, seq, num_heads, head_size, rotary_ndims); })); } """ cpp_sources = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> void apply_rotary_cuda(at::Tensor qkv, at::Tensor cos, at::Tensor sin, int rotary_ndims); at::Tensor forward_fast( at::Tensor input_ids, at::Tensor embed_weight, at::Tensor input_ln_weights, at::Tensor input_ln_biases, at::Tensor qkv_weights, at::Tensor qkv_biases, at::Tensor dense_weights, at::Tensor dense_biases, at::Tensor post_ln_weights, at::Tensor post_ln_biases, at::Tensor mlp_h_weights, at::Tensor mlp_h_biases, at::Tensor mlp_out_weights, at::Tensor mlp_out_biases, at::Tensor final_ln_weight, at::Tensor final_ln_bias, at::Tensor lm_head_weight, at::Tensor lm_head_bias, at::Tensor cos_cached, at::Tensor sin_cached, bool use_parallel_residual, double layer_norm_eps, int rotary_ndims, int num_heads) { auto hidden_states = at::embedding(embed_weight, input_ids); int batch = hidden_states.size(0); int seq = hidden_states.size(1); int hidden_size = hidden_states.size(2); int num_layers = input_ln_weights.size(0); int head_size = hidden_size / num_heads; for (int i = 0; i < num_layers; ++i) { auto ln1_w = input_ln_weights.select(0, i); auto ln1_b = input_ln_biases.select(0, i); auto qkv_w = qkv_weights.select(0, i); auto qkv_b = qkv_biases.select(0, i); auto d_w = dense_weights.select(0, i); auto d_b = dense_biases.select(0, i); auto ln2_w = post_ln_weights.select(0, i); auto ln2_b = post_ln_biases.select(0, i); auto mlp_h_w = mlp_h_weights.select(0, i); auto mlp_h_b = mlp_h_biases.select(0, i); auto mlp_out_w = mlp_out_weights.select(0, i); auto mlp_out_b = mlp_out_biases.select(0, i); auto residual = hidden_states; // Attention auto attn_input = at::layer_norm(hidden_states, {hidden_size}, ln1_w, ln1_b, layer_norm_eps); auto qkv = at::addmm(qkv_b, attn_input, qkv_w.t()); qkv = qkv.reshape({batch, seq, 3, num_heads, head_size}); apply_rotary_cuda(qkv, cos_cached, sin_cached, rotary_ndims); auto q = qkv.select(2, 0).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).contiguous(); auto k = qkv.select(2, 1).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).contiguous(); auto v = qkv.select(2, 2).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).contiguous(); auto attn_out = at::scaled_dot_product_attention(q, k, v, c10::nullopt, 0.0, true); attn_out = attn_out.permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).reshape({batch, seq, hidden_size}); attn_out = at::addmm(d_b, attn_out, d_w.t()); if (use_parallel_residual) { auto mlp_input = at::layer_norm(hidden_states, {hidden_size}, ln2_w, ln2_b, layer_norm_eps); auto mlp_h = at::addmm(mlp_h_b, mlp_input, mlp_h_w.t()); auto mlp_h_act = at::gelu(mlp_h); auto mlp_out = at::addmm(mlp_out_b, mlp_h_act, mlp_out_w.t()); hidden_states = mlp_out + attn_out + residual; } else { auto ff_input = at::layer_norm(residual + attn_out, {hidden_size}, ln2_w, ln2_b, layer_norm_eps); auto mlp_h = at::addmm(mlp_h_b, ff_input, mlp_h_w.t()); auto mlp_h_act = at::gelu(mlp_h); auto mlp_out = at::addmm(mlp_out_b, mlp_h_act, mlp_out_w.t()); hidden_states = mlp_out + residual + attn_out; } } hidden_states = at::layer_norm(hidden_states, {hidden_size}, final_ln_weight, final_ln_bias, layer_norm_eps); auto logits = at::addmm(lm_head_bias, hidden_states, lm_head_weight.t()); return logits; } """ _gptneox_fast = load_inline( name="gptneox_fast", cpp_sources=cpp_sources, cuda_sources=cuda_sources, functions=["forward_fast"], extra_cflags=["-O3"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], verbose=False, ) import math from transformers import AutoModelForCausalLM class ModelNew(torch.nn.Module): def __init__(self, model_name, config): super().__init__() self.model_name = model_name self.config = config # Load the pretrained model to extract weights model = gpt_neox.final_layer_norm.weight.detach()) self.register_buffer("final_ln_bias", gpt_neox.final_layer_norm.bias.detach()) # LM head lm_head = model.embed_out self.register_buffer("lm_head_weight", dtype=lm_head.weight.dtype, device=lm_head.weight.device)) num_layers = config.num_hidden_layers hidden_size = config.hidden_size intermediate_size = config.intermediate_size # Collect layer weights qkv_b.append(layer.attention.query_key_value.bias.detach()) dense_w.append(layer.attention.dense.weight.detach()) dense_b.append(layer.attention.dense.bias.detach()) post_ln_w.append(layer.post_attention_layernorm.weight.detach()) post_ln_b.append(layer.post_attention_layernorm.bias.detach()) mlp_h_w.append(layer.mlp.dense_h_to_4h.weight.detach()) mlp_h_b.append(layer.mlp.dense_h_to_4h.bias.detach()) mlp_out_w.append(layer.mlp.dense_4h_to_h.weight.detach()) mlp_out_b.append(layer.mlp.dense_4h_to_h.bias.detach()) # Stack and register self.register_buffer("input_ln_weights", torch.stack(input_ln_w, 0)) self.register_buffer("input_ln_biases", torch.stack(input_ln_b, 0)) self.register_buffer("qkv_weights", torch.stack(qkv_w, 0)) self.register_buffer("qkv_biases", torch.stack(qkv_b, 0)) self.register_buffer("dense_weights", torch.stack(dense_w, 0)) self.register_buffer("dense_biases", torch.stack(dense_b, 0)) self.register_buffer("post_ln_weights", torch.stack(post_ln_w, 0)) self.register_buffer("post_ln_biases", torch.stack(post_ln_b, 0)) self.register_buffer("mlp_h_weights", torch.stack(mlp_h_w, 0)) self.register_buffer("mlp_h_biases", torch.stack(mlp_h_b, 0)) self.register_buffer("mlp_out_weights", torch.stack(mlp_out_w, 0)) self.register_buffer("mlp_out_biases", torch.stack(mlp_out_b, 0)) # Rotary cache rotary_ndims = int((hidden_size // config.num_attention_heads) * getattr(config, "rotary_pct", 0.25)) max_pos = config.max_position_embeddings inv_freq = 1.0 / (10000 ** (torch.arange(0, rotary_ndims, 2, dtype=torch.float32) / rotary_ndims)) t = torch.arange(max_pos, dtype=torch.float32) freqs = torch.einsum("i,j->ij", t, inv_freq) emb = torch.cat((freqs, freqs), dim=-1) cos_cached = emb.cos().to(dtype=embed_weight.dtype) sin_cached = emb.sin().to(dtype=embed_weight.dtype) self.register_buffer("cos_cached", cos_cached) self.register_buffer("sin_cached", sin_cached) self.use_parallel_residual = getattr(config, "use_parallel_residual", False) self.layer_norm_eps = getattr(config, "layer_norm_eps", 1e-5) self.rotary_ndims = rotary_ndims self.num_heads = self.rotary_ndims, self.num_heads, ) return logits ``` This is the full code. \"rotary_pct\", 0.25)\n rotary_ndims = int(head_size * rotary_pct)\n max_pos = config.max_position_embeddings\n inv_freq = 1.0 / (10000 ** (torch.arange(0, rotary_ndims, 2, dtype=torch.float32) / rotary_ndims))\n t = torch.arange(max_pos, dtype=torch.float32)\n freqs = torch.einsum(\"i,j->ij\", t, inv_freq)\n emb = torch.cat((freqs, freqs), dim=-1)\n cos_cached = emb.cos().to(dtype=self.embed_weight.dtype)\n sin_cached = emb.sin().to(dtype=self.embed_weight.dtype)\n self.register_buffer(\"cos_cached\", cos_cached)\n self.register_buffer(\"sin_cached\", sin_cached)\n\n self.use_parallel_residual = getattr(config, \"use_parallel_residual\", False)\n self.layer_norm_eps = getattr(config, \"layer_norm_eps\", 1e-5)\n self.rotary_ndims = rotary_ndims\n The standard RoPE in transformers uses `rotate_half` which rotates by *negating the second half and concatenating with the first half*. It does not include any extra layers or wrappers. Are there any? In `GPTNeoXForCausalLM`, the `forward` method computes `hidden_states = self.gpt_neox(...)[0]`, then `lm_logits = self.embed_out(hidden_states)`. My code does the same. Are there any tied weights? In Pythia, `embed_out.weight` is not tied to `embed_in.weight` (I think they are separate). Let me check: for GPT-NeoX, `embed_out` is a separate linear layer. In my code, I register `embed_weight` and `lm_head_weight` separately. If they are tied, I would register two copies of the same tensor. Wait, if they are tied, `model.embed_out.weight` and `model.gpt_neox.embed_in.weight` point to the same tensor. My code would register two separate buffers with the same data. That's fine. Another thing: the `lm_head_bias`. In GPT-NeoX, `embed_out` is `nn.Linear`, which has a bias if `bias=True`. I handle that. Now, a big performance optimization: since I'm re-implementing the forward pass in C++, I should profile to see if it's faster than the PyTorch reference. The PyTorch reference likely uses FlashAttention-2 via `torch.nn.functional.scaled_dot_product_attention`. My code also uses `at::scaled_dot_product_attention`, which PyTorch will dispatch to FlashAttention if available. So the attention part should be similar. The main speedup potential comes from: 1. Fusing layers or reducing Python overhead. 2. Eliminating intermediate temporaries by keeping everything in C++. 3. Using a custom fused rotary kernel instead of the Python one. Since I'm doing the entire forward pass in a single C++ loop, there's much less Python overhead per layer. For a 24-layer model, this could save significant time. Also, my custom rotary kernel might be slightly faster than the Python one. But wait, for a 1.4B model with batch=8 and seq=512, the main bottleneck is likely the GEMMs (matrix multiplications). Moving the loop to C++ won't change the GEMM performance since they call the same cuBLAS kernels. However, Python overhead can be non-negligible for many layers. Also, my implementation reduces the number of intermediate Python objects. Another potential optimization: pre-transpose the weights and store them as buffers. In `nn.Linear`, `weight` is `[out_features, in_features]`. `at::linear` uses `weight.t()` internally, which might create a transpose view. But cuBLAS can handle this without an explicit transpose copy. However, for many calls, it might be slightly better to store weights in a layout that matches the matmul. But not necessary. Wait, there is a known issue: `at::linear` in C++ might call `at::matmul` + `add_`, which is slightly different from `at::addmm` used in optimized paths. But it should still use cuBLAS for the matmul. Actually, `at::linear` in PyTorch is implemented as: ```python def linear(input, weight, bias=None): if input.dim() == 2 and bias is not None: # fused path return torch.addmm(bias, input, weight.t()) output = input.matmul(weight.t()) if bias is not None: output += bias return output ``` Wait, in C++, `at::native::linear` does something similar. For 3D input, it calls `matmul`, which dispatches to `bmm`. For 2D, it calls `addmm`. So the performance should be fine. Now, considering all of this, my approach should be correct and potentially faster due to reduced Python overhead. But I need to make sure there's no subtle accuracy issue. One subtle issue: `at::layer_norm` in C++ might have slightly different numerics than Python `LayerNorm`. But `LayerNorm` in Python calls the same C++ implementation (`torch.nn.functional.layer_norm`), so they should match. Another subtle issue: `at::gelu` in C++. The default `gelu` in PyTorch uses `approximate='none'`. In GPT-NeoX, the activation is `gelu` (not `gelu_new` or `gelu_fast`? Actually, GPT-NeoX uses `gelu` I think). Let me check: in `transformers`, GPTNeoXMLP uses `self.act = ACT2FN[config.hidden_act]`. For GPT-NeoX, `hidden_act` is typically `"gelu"`. `ACT2FN["gelu"]` maps to `GELUActivation()`, which is `nn.GELU(approximate='none')`. So `at::gelu` is correct (`at::gelu` corresponds to exact GELU). Wait, is `at::gelu` in the C++ API exact or approximate? `at::gelu` in PyTorch C++ takes a string parameter for approximate mode in newer versions, but the default might be `'none'` or `'tanh'`. Actually, in PyTorch 2.x, `at::gelu(input)` without a second argument uses the `'none'` approximation? Let me check. In PyTorch C++: ```cpp Tensor gelu(const Tensor& self, c10::string_view approximate) { return at::native::gelu(self, approximate); } ``` But there might be overloads. Actually, `at::gelu(const Tensor& self)` might call `gelu(self, "none")` or `gelu(self, "tanh")` depending on version. Wait! This is important. In older PyTorch, `gelu` without approximate used "none". In newer PyTorch (2.0+), there might be a default. But let's check `nn.GELU`: in PyTorch 2.0, `nn.GELU()` defaults to `approximate='none'`. The C++ `at::gelu` should match. But to be safe, I should check if there is a two-argument version. Actually, `at::gelu` in `native_functions.yaml` has: ``` - func: gelu(Tensor self, str approximate='none') -> Tensor ``` So calling `at::gelu(mlp_h)` uses default `'none'`. Correct. Another subtle issue: `at::scaled_dot_product_attention(q, k, v, c10::nullopt, 0.0, true)` - the `is_causal` boolean. Does it match the causal mask used in the reference? In GPT-NeoX, `causal_mask` is typically a lower-triangular boolean mask added to attention scores. `is_causal=True` in SDPA does the same. But there's a catch: in `transformers` GPTNeoX, sometimes `attention_mask` is passed. Since `get_inputs()` does not pass `attention_mask`, the reference uses `causal_mask` only. My `sdpa` with `is_causal=True` also uses causal mask only. Now, what about the `position_ids`? In GPT-NeoX, rotary embeddings need position ids. If not passed, they default to `torch.arange(seq_len)`. My `cos_cached` and `sin_cached` are precomputed for all positions `0..max_pos-1`, and `apply_rotary_cuda` uses `s` (the sequence index) to look up the cos/sin. So it assumes positions are `0..seq-1`. This is correct when no explicit `position_ids` are provided. All right, let me implement the fixes and submit. Full code: ```python import torch from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_sources = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void apply_rotary_kernel( scalar_t* __restrict__ qkv, const scalar_t* __restrict__ cos, const scalar_t* __restrict__ sin, int batch, int seq, int num_heads, int head_size, int rotary_ndims) { int half_rotary = rotary_ndims / 2; int total = batch * seq * num_heads * half_rotary; int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= total) return; int pair = idx % half_rotary; int head = (idx / half_rotary) % num_heads; int s = (idx / (half_rotary * num_heads)) % seq; int b = idx / (half_rotary * num_heads * seq); int stride_bs = 3 * num_heads * head_size; int base = ((b * seq + s)) * stride_bs; int offset_head = head * head_size + pair; int offset_q = base + 0 * num_heads * head_size + offset_head; int offset_k = base + 1 * num_heads * head_size + offset_head; int offset_q2 = offset_q + half_rotary; int offset_k2 = offset_k + half_rotary; int cs_off = s * rotary_ndims + pair; scalar_t q1 = qkv[offset_q]; scalar_t q2 = qkv[offset_q2]; scalar_t k1 = qkv[offset_k]; scalar_t qkv[offset_k2] = k1 * s_val + k2 * c; } void apply_rotary_cuda(at::Tensor qkv, at::Tensor cos, at::Tensor sin, int rotary_ndims) { int batch = qkv.size(0); int seq = qkv.size(1); int num_heads = qkv.size(3); int head_size = qkv.size(4); int half_rotary = rotary_ndims / 2; int total = batch * seq * num_heads * half_rotary; if (total == 0) return; const int threads = 256; const int blocks = (total + threads - 1) / threads; AT_DISPATCH_FLOATING_TYPES(qkv.scalar_type(), "apply_rotary", ([&] { apply_rotary_kernel<scalar_t><<<blocks, threads>>>( qkv.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), i); auto mlp_out_b = mlp_out_biases.select(0, i); auto residual = hidden_states; // Attention path auto attn_input = at::layer_norm(hidden_states, {hidden_size}, ln1_w, ln1_b, layer_norm_eps); auto 0).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).contiguous(); auto k = qkv.select(2, 1).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).contiguous(); auto v = qkv.select(2, 2).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).contiguous(); auto attn_out = at::scaled_dot_product_attention(q, k, v, c10::nullopt, 0.0, true); attn_out = attn_out.permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).reshape({batch, seq, hidden_size}); attn_out = at::linear(attn_out, d_w, d_b); if (use_parallel_residual) { auto mlp_input = at::layer_norm(hidden_states, {hidden_size}, ln2_w, ln2_b, layer_norm_eps); auto mlp_h = at::linear(mlp_input, mlp_h_w, mlp_h_b); auto mlp_h_act = at::gelu(mlp_h); auto mlp_out = at::linear(mlp_h_act, mlp_out_w, mlp_out_b); hidden_states = mlp_out + attn_out + residual; } else { auto ff_input = at::layer_norm(residual + attn_out, {hidden_size}, ln2_w, ln2_b, layer_norm_eps); auto mlp_h = at::linear(ff_input, mlp_h_w, mlp_h_b); auto mlp_h_act = at::gelu(mlp_h); auto mlp_out = at::linear(mlp_h_act, mlp_out_w, mlp_out_b); hidden_states = self.register_buffer("lm_head_bias", lm_head.bias.detach()) else: self.register_buffer( "lm_head_bias", torch.zeros(lm_head.weight.size(0), dtype=lm_head.weight.dtype, device=lm_head.weight.device), ) # Collect layer parameters input_ln_w, input_ln_b = [], [] qkv_w, qkv_b = [], [] dense_w, dense_b = [], [] post_ln_w, post_ln_b = [], [] mlp_h_w, mlp_h_b = [], [] mlp_out_w, mlp_out_b = [], [] for i in range(num_layers): layer = gpt_neox.layers[i] input_ln_w.append(layer.input_layernorm.weight.detach()) input_ln_b.append(layer.input_layernorm.bias.detach()) qkv_w.append(layer.attention.query_key_value.weight.detach()) qkv_b.append(layer.attention.query_key_value.bias.detach()) dense_w.append(layer.attention.dense.weight.detach()) dense_b.append(layer.attention.dense.bias.detach()) post_ln_w.append(layer.post_attention_layernorm.weight.detach()) post_ln_b.append(layer.post_attention_layernorm.bias.detach()) mlp_h_w.append(layer.mlp.dense_h_to_4h.weight.detach()) mlp_h_b.append(layer.mlp.dense_h_to_4h.bias.detach()) mlp_out_w.append(layer.mlp.dense_4h_to_h.weight.detach()) mlp_out_b.append(layer.mlp.dense_4h_to_h.bias.detach()) self.register_buffer("input_ln_weights", torch.stack(input_ln_w, 0)) self.register_buffer("input_ln_biases", torch.stack(input_ln_b, 0)) self.register_buffer("qkv_weights", torch.stack(qkv_w, 0)) self.register_buffer("qkv_biases", torch.stack(qkv_b, 0)) self.register_buffer("dense_weights", torch.stack(dense_w, 0)) self.register_buffer("dense_biases", torch.stack(dense_b, 0)) self.register_buffer("post_ln_weights", torch.stack(post_ln_w, 0)) self.register_buffer("post_ln_biases", torch.stack(post_ln_b, 0)) 0.25) rotary_ndims = int(head_size * rotary_pct) max_pos = config.max_position_embeddings inv_freq = 1.0 / (10000 ** (torch.arange(0, rotary_ndims, 2, dtype=torch.float32) / rotary_ndims)) t = torch.arange(max_pos, dtype=torch.float32) freqs = torch.einsum("i,j->ij", t, inv_freq) emb = torch.cat((freqs, freqs), dim=-1) cos_cached = emb.cos().to(dtype=self.embed_weight.dtype) sin_cached = emb.sin().to(dtype=self.embed_weight.dtype) self.register_buffer("cos_cached", cos_cached) self.register_buffer("sin_cached", sin_cached) self.use_parallel_residual = getattr(config, "use_parallel_residual", False) self.layer_norm_eps = getattr(config, "layer_norm_eps", 1e-5) self.rotary_ndims = rotary_ndims self.num_heads = config.num_attention_heads del model def forward(self,
